Ruling on Selling Olives for Olive Oil and Similar Ribawi Transactions
Sets conditions for permissible sale of oil-bearing fruits for their oil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid sale of an oil-bearing fruit for its extracted oil, apply the rule to analogous cases, and identify when riba fadl occurs.

Core Idea
Selling fruit for its oil is only permissible when the oil given exceeds the fruit's oil content, ensuring the excess is payment for the residue.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a common transaction: selling a fruit (e.g., olives) for its extracted oil. Since both the fruit's oil and the pure oil are of the same genus and weighed, the sale triggers riba rules. The Hanafi school sets a precise condition: the pure oil given must exceed the oil content of the fruit. This ensures the oil-for-oil exchange is equal, and the surplus compensates for the residue (tajir), which is a different genus. If the oil content is unknown, the sale is also prohibited due to shubhat al-riba. The ruling extends to walnuts for oil, milk for ghee, grapes for juice, and dates for molasses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and its riba risk
Recognize that the sale involves two goods of the same genus (oil) that are both weighed. Hanafi law requires equality in such exchanges to avoid riba fadl. The fruit contains oil and residue; the pure oil is only oil.
The fruit is not a pure substance; it has oil and non-oil components.
2
State the condition for validity
The pure oil given must be greater than the oil contained in the fruit. This ensures that the oil portion of the fruit is exchanged for an equal amount of pure oil, and the extra pure oil is payment for the residue (tajir), which is a different genus.
Tajir is not ribawi with oil, so disparity is allowed.
3
Identify invalidating factors
If the oil in the fruit equals or exceeds the pure oil given, the residue becomes an uncompensated extra, constituting riba fadl. Additionally, if the oil content of the fruit is unknown, the sale is invalid due to the possibility (shubha) of riba.
Shubha in riba is treated like actual riba.
4
Apply to analogous cases
The same rule applies to walnuts for their oil, milk for ghee, grapes for juice, and dates for molasses. The key is that the extracted product (oil/ghee/juice/molasses) is the same genus as the component in the original item.
The residue in each case is a different genus.
5
Note the consensus exception
Cloth (cotton fabric) sold for cotton is permissible in any manner by consensus, because cloth is a processed good of a different genus (manufactured vs. raw material). The ikhtilaf on cotton for its yarn is noted but not detailed.
This shows how processing can change genus for riba purposes.

ہدایہ
He said: And it is not permissible to sell olives for olive oil, nor sesame for sesame oil, unless the olive oil and sesame oil are more than what is in the olives and sesame, so that the oil (in the fruit) is equivalent to the oil (given), and the excess is for the residue (tajir). Because then it becomes free of riba, since what is in it (the fruit) of oil is weighed. And this is because if what is in it were more or equal to it, then the residue and some oil, or the residue alone, would be excess. And if the amount of what is in it is not known, it is not permissible due to the possibility of riba, and doubt (shubha) in riba is like the reality. And walnuts for their oil, milk for its ghee, grapes for their juice, and dates for their molasses are on the same basis. And they differed regarding cotton for its yarn, and cloth for cotton is permissible in any way by consensus. Translation: And selling olives for olive oil, and sesame for its oil, is not permissible unless the olive oil and sesame oil are more than what is in the olives and sesame, so that the oil (in the fruit) is the equivalent of the oil (given), and the excess is for the residue (tajir). Because then the contract becomes free of riba, since the oil that is in the olives or sesame is a weighed thing. And this condition of excess is because if the oil in the olives or sesame were more or equal to the pure oil, then the residue and a part of the oil, or the residue alone, would be excess. And if the quantity of oil in the olives or oil is not known, it is also not permissible due to the possibility of riba, and doubt in riba is like its reality. And walnuts for their oil, milk for its ghee, grapes for their juice, and dates for their molasses are on the same basis. And they differed regarding cotton for its yarn, and cloth for cotton is permissible in any way by consensus. Explanation: Olives are the fruit from which olive oil is extracted. Shiraj is the oil extracted from sesame. Tajir means the residue (pulp). The ruling: If olives are sold for olive oil, or sesame for its oil, and the pure oil (given) is more than the oil contained in the fruit, then the sale is permissible because riba does not occur. Riba does not occur because the pure oil is exchanged for its equivalent in the fruit, and the excess pure oil is paid for by the residue (tajir), which is a different genus. If the oil in the fruit is more or equal to the pure oil, the sale is not permissible. Because the pure oil is also weighed, and the oil in the fruit is weighed, so due to genus and measure, both countervalues must be equal. When the oil in the fruit exceeds the pure oil, one countervalue contains extra oil and residue; if equal, the residue is extra. In both cases, one side has excess, which is riba. If the quantity of oil in the fruit is unknown, the sale is also not permissible due to possibility of riba, as doubt in riba is prohibited like actual riba.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed ruling on selling oil-bearing fruits (olives, sesame) for their extracted oils. The key principle is that such sales are permissible only when the pure oil given as payment is greater than the oil content in the fruit. This ensures that the oil portion of the fruit is exchanged for an equal amount of pure oil, and the excess pure oil is in fact payment for the residue (tajir), which is a different genus. If the oil in the fruit is equal to or greater than the pure oil, riba occurs because the residue becomes an uncompensated extra. If the oil content is unknown, the sale is also invalid due to the likelihood of riba, as shubha (doubt) in riba is treated like actual riba. The author then extends the same ruling to other analogous cases: walnuts for their oil, milk for ghee, grapes for juice, dates for molasses. The passage ends with a note of ikhtilaf (disagreement) regarding cotton for its yarn, but cloth for cotton is unanimously permissible in any manner. This passage emphasizes the Hanafi strictness in avoiding riba in transactions involving goods that can be processed into other forms but retain a common genus (oil, etc.)

Key Terms
زیتون (zaytun): Olives, the fruit from which olive oil is extracted.
شیرج (shiraj): Sesame oil, extracted from sesame seeds.
تجیر (tajir): Residue or pulp left after extracting oil; considered a different genus from the oil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling olives for olive oil prohibited if the oil in the olives is equal to the pure olive oil given?
A) Because the olives themselves are not ribawi.
B) Because the pure oil is not weighed.
C) Because the residue (tajir) would be extra, constituting riba fadl.
D) Because the quantity of oil in olives is always unknown.

Key Takeaway
For a valid sale of fruit for its oil, the pure oil given must exceed the fruit's oil content, and the fruit's oil content must be known, to eliminate riba.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Selling Fruits for Their Extracted Oils and Juices: Conditions to Avoid Riba
Ruling with conditions for selling fruits for their extracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling olives for olive oil and apply the condition of excess to determine the validity of similar exchanges.

Core Idea
A sale of fruit for its extracted product is valid only if the extract given exceeds the content in the fruit.

When you sell olives for olive oil, you are exchanging two things that belong to the same genus (oil) and are both measured by weight. The Hanafi school is particularly strict about riba al-fadl in such exchanges. This passage from the Hidayah explains the precise condition that must be satisfied to keep the transaction free from riba: the oil you give must be more than the oil inside the fruit. The same logic applies to sesame oil, walnut oil, milk butter, grape juice, and date molasses. There is also an interesting difference of opinion among later Hanafi scholars about cotton and yarn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
The passage begins by stating that selling olives for olive oil is not permissible (la yajuz) unless a condition is met. This tells you the default ruling: impermissible due to riba risk.
The default is fasid (voidable) if conditions are violated.
2
Locate the condition for validity
The condition is that the oil given in exchange must be more than the oil contained in the fruit. The excess is considered payment for the residue (tajir), which is of a different genus and therefore allows disparity.
The residue is a different genus, so disparity is allowed.
3
Understand the rationale (talil)
The reasoning is based on riba al-fadl: same genus, same measure. If the oil given equals or is less than the oil in the fruit, then either residue alone or residue plus some oil becomes extra without countervalue, constituting riba. Also, if the quantity in the fruit is unknown, the sale is impermissible due to shubha (doubt), which is treated like actual riba.
Hanafis treat shubha in riba as severely as certainty.
4
Apply the condition to analogous cases and note the ikhtilaf
The same ruling applies to walnuts for oil, milk for butter, grapes for juice, dates for molasses. Then the passage discusses cotton for yarn: some Hanafi scholars permit it (same origin, both weighed), others forbid it (analogy to wheat and flour). This shows how the condition is applied analogically.
The ikhtilaf is intra-Hanafi; no clear tarjih is given.

کھجور کی بیع اس کے شیرہ کے عوض اسی اعتبار پر جائز ہے یعنی اگر خالص تیل اس سے زائد ہو جو انگور میں ہے اور کھجور کا خالص شیرہ اس سے زائد ہو جو کھجور میں ہے تو یہ بیع جائز ہے ورنہ نہیں۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ روئی کو اس کے سوت کے عوض وزن کے اعتبار سے برابر کر کے بیچنے میں اختلاف ہے چنانچہ بعض مشائخ جواز کے قائل ہیں اور جواز کی دلیل یہ بیان کرتے ہیں کہ روئی اور سوت دونوں کی اصل ایک ہے یعنی روئی کی اصل بھی کپاس ہے اور سوت کی اصل بھی کپاس ہے اور دونوں موزونی ہیں۔ پس اتحاد جنس اور اتحاد قدر دونوں پائے گئے۔ اس لئے روئی کو سوت کے عوض برابر برابر بیچنے میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے۔ اور بعض مشائخ کا قول ہے کہ روئی کو سوت کے عوض برابر کر کے بیچنا بھی جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ روئی کو کات کر سوت بنانے سے روئی کم ہو جاتی ہے۔ مثلاً ایک کلو روئی کو کات کر اگر سوت بنایا گیا تو یہ ایک کلو نہ ہوگا بلکہ ایک کلو سے کم ہو جائے گا۔ جیسے گندم کو آٹے کے عوض برابر برابر بیچنا جائز نہیں ہے اور سوتی کپڑے کو روئی کے عوض بیچنا ہر طرح جائز ہے خواہ کپڑے کی روئی سے یہ روئی زیادہ ہو۔ خواہ برابر ہو خواہ کم ہو۔ اور اس پر اجماع ہے کیونکہ سوتی کپڑے اور روئی کی جنس مختلف ہیں اس لئے کمی زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنے میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے۔
He said: It is not permissible to sell olives for olive oil, nor sesame for sesame oil, unless the oil (olive oil) and sesame oil are more than what is in the olives and sesame, so that the oil (extracted) is equivalent to the oil (in the fruit) and the excess is for the residue (al-tajīr), because then the transaction is free from ribā, since the oil in them is a weighed commodity. This is because if the oil in them were more or equal, then there would be an excess of residue and some oil, or residue alone. And if the quantity in them is unknown, it is not permissible due to the possibility of ribā, and uncertainty (shubha) in ribā is like certainty. The same applies to selling walnuts for their oil, milk for its butter, grapes for their juice, and dates for their molasses on this basis. And they differed concerning cotton for its yarn, and coarse cotton cloth (karbās) for cotton is permissible in any case by consensus. Translation: And selling olives for olive oil, and sesame for its oil, is not permissible unless the olive oil and sesame oil are more than what is in the olives and sesame, so that the oil is equivalent to the oil, and the excess is for the residue (khalī). Because then the contract is free from ribā, since the oil in the olives or sesame is a weighed commodity. This condition is imposed because if the oil in them were more or equal to the oil (given in exchange), then either residue and some oil, or residue alone, would be excess. And if the quantity of oil in them is unknown, it is also not permissible due to the possibility of ribā, and shubha in ribā is like reality. The same principle applies to selling walnuts for their oil, milk for its butter, grapes for their juice, and dates for their molasses. And there is disagreement regarding selling cotton for its yarn. And selling cotton cloth for cotton is permissible in any way by consensus. Explanation: Zaytūn is the fruit from which olive oil is extracted, and shīraj is the oil extracted from sesame. Mujīr means residue. The case is that if olives are sold for olive oil or sesame for its oil, and the olive oil (given) is more than the oil in the olives, and the pure oil (given) is more than the oil in the sesame, then the sale is permissible, because ribā does not occur. Ribā does not occur because the pure oil becomes equivalent to the oil in the olives or sesame, and the excess quantity of pure oil is in exchange for the residue. Since residue and oil are of different genres, no ribā occurs. And if the oil in the olives or sesame is more than or equal to the pure oil (given), then the sale is not permissible. Because pure oil is also weighed, and the oil in the olives and sesame is also weighed; therefore, due to the presence of genus and measure, equality of both countervalues is necessary. But when the oil in the olives or sesame is more than the pure oil (extracted), then in that case one countervalue includes some oil and residue as excess, and if the oil inside is equal to the pure oil, then residue alone is excess. The excess of one countervalue and the deficiency of the other is called ribā. Thus, in both cases, since ribā is inevitable, these two forms are impermissible. And if it is unknown how much oil is in the olives or sesame, then also the sale is impermissible, because there is a possibility of ribā, and shubha in ribā is prohibited like actual ribā. Therefore, even in the case of unknown quantity, the sale is impermissible. The author of Hidāyah says: Selling walnuts for their oil, milk for its butter, grapes for their juice, and dates for their molasses is permissible on the same basis: i.e., if the pure oil is more than that in the grapes, and the pure juice of dates is more than that in the dates, then the sale is permissible; otherwise not. The author of Hidāyah says: There is disagreement regarding selling cotton for its yarn by weight equally. Some mashāyikh hold it permissible, giving the proof that both cotton and yarn have the same origin, i.e., both come from cotton, and both are weighed. Thus, unity of genus and unity of measure are present, so selling cotton for yarn equally is fine. Other mashāyikh say it is not permissible, because when cotton is spun into yarn, the cotton becomes less. For example, one kilo of cotton spun into yarn will not be one kilo but less. Similarly, selling wheat for flour equally is not permissible. And selling cotton cloth for cotton is permissible in any case, whether the cloth's cotton is more, equal, or less, and there is consensus on this, because the genre of cotton cloth and cotton is different; hence, selling with disparity is fine.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hidāyah on the conditions for selling a fruit or commodity for its own extracted product (e.g., olives for olive oil) to avoid ribā al-fadl. The general rule: when two items are of the same genus and both are measured by weight or volume, exchange must be equal. Here, the fruit contains the same substance (oil) as the extracted product; thus, they are of the same genus. The fruit also has residue (tajīr) which is of a different genus, so disparity is allowed but only if the extracted product is more than the substance in the fruit, so that the oil matches exactly and the excess is for the residue. If the oil in the fruit is equal or more, ribā occurs because the residue alone or with some oil becomes an excess without countervalue. The passage then applies the same logic to other similar pairs (walnuts, milk, grapes, dates). Then it notes an intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf regarding cotton and yarn: some scholars analogize to wheat and flour (disallowing) while others point to unity of origin and allow. However, selling cotton cloth for cotton is unanimously permissible due to difference in genre. The author emphasizes that shubha (doubt) in ribā is treated like actual ribā, so even if the quantity of oil in the fruit is unknown, the sale is impermissible. This demonstrates the strict Hanafi stance on avoiding ribā even at the level of uncertainty.

Key Terms
ربوا (ribā): Usury or interest, specifically ribā al-fadl (excess) in exchange of same genus commodities.
مثل (mithl): Equivalence in quantity (weight or measure) for the same genus to avoid ribā.
جنس (jins): Genus or species; here, it determines whether ribā applies. Same jins requires equality.
تجير (tajīr): Residue or pomace left after extracting oil from olives or sesame; a non-ribawi item allowing disparity.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt or uncertainty; in riba, shubha is treated like actual riba, prohibiting transactions with potential riba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if someone sells 1 kg of olives for 1 kg of olive oil, and the olives contain 0.3 kg of oil, is the sale permissible?
A) Yes, because the oil is of the same genus and equal in weight.
B) No, because the oil in the olives is unknown, so the sale is not permissible.
C) Yes, because the residue (tajir) is a different genus and allows disparity.
D) No, because the olive oil given (1 kg) is more than the oil in the olives (0.3 kg), so the condition of excess is not met? Actually it is met, but the equality condition applies? Wait, let's think.

Key Takeaway
When selling a fruit for its extracted product, the extract given must exceed the content in the fruit to avoid riba; otherwise, the sale is fasid.

Exchanging Different Meats: Disparity and Genus
Ruling on exchanging different meats with inequality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on exchanging different meats with disparity and apply the genus-determination rule using zakat nisāb.

Core Idea
Meat exchange: different species, disparity allowed; same species, equality required — genus determined by zakat nisāb.

In Hanafi law, ribā al-fadl prohibits exchanging food items of the same genus in unequal amounts. Meat, being a weighed food, falls under this rule. The Hidāyah clarifies which meats are considered same genus, and the ʿInāyah provides a practical criterion: if the zakat nisāb of one animal can be completed by another, they are same genus. This lesson will equip you to distinguish between permissible and impermissible meat exchanges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The text rules that selling different meats with disparity (tafāḍul) is permissible. The key condition is that the meats must be of different genera (jins).
If the meats are of the same genus, disparity is prohibited due to ribā.
2
Determine genus using the given examples
The author specifies that cow and buffalo are one genus, as are goat and sheep, and Arabian and Bactrian camel. Meats across these groups (e.g., cow and camel) are different genera.
The examples clarify the scope of 'different meats'.
3
Apply the ʿInāyah principle for unknown cases
When uncertain, use the rule: if the zakat nisāb of one animal can be completed by another, they are same genus. For instance, cows and buffaloes share a nisāb (30 each), so they are same genus.
This rule links zakat and ribā rulings.
4
Distinguish permissible from prohibited exchanges
Apply the genus test: if meats are of different genera, disparity is allowed; if same genus, equality is required.
Practical outcome: e.g., 1 kg cow meat for 1.2 kg camel meat is allowed; 1 kg cow for 1.2 kg buffalo is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ويجوز بيع اللحمان المختلفة بعضها ببعض متفاضلا ومراده لحم الإبل والبقر والغنم فأما البقر والجواميس جنس واحد وكذا المعز مع الضان وكذا العراب مع البخاتي ترجمہ اور مختلف گوشتوں میں سے بعض کو بعض کے عوض کمی زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنا جائز ہے اور اس کی مراد اونٹ کا گوشت، گائے کا گوشت اور بکری کا گوشت ہے۔ اور گائے اور بھینس کا گوشت ایک ہی جنس ہے۔ اور اسی طرح بکری اور بھیڑ اور عربی اور بختی ایک ہی جنس ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر گوشت مختلف الا جناس ہوں مثلاً اونٹ کا گوشت ہے، گائے کا گوشت ہے اور بکری کا گوشت ہے۔ ان میں سے اگر ایک کو دوسرے کے عوض کمی زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچا گیا تو جائز ہے کیونکہ گائے، اونٹ اور بکری کے گوشت کی جنس الگ الگ ہے۔ اور اختلاف جنس کی صورت میں چونکہ کمی زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنا جائز ہو گا۔ اور گائے اور بھینس دونوں کی ایک جنس ہے لہذا ان میں سے ایک کے گوشت کو دوسرے کے عوض کمی زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنا جائز نہیں ہے اور اسی طرح بکری اور بھیڑ کی ایک جنس ہے اور عربی اونٹ اور بختی اونٹ کی ایک جنس ہے تو بکری کے گوشت کو بھیڑ کے گوشت کے عوض اور عربی اونٹ کے گوشت کو بختی اونٹ کے گوشت کے عوض کمی زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ صاحب عنایہ نے حیوانات میں اتحاد جنس اور اختلاف جنس کے سلسلہ میں ایک ضابطہ بیان فرمایا ہے وہ یہ کہ زکوۃ میں اگر ایک جانور کا نصاب دوسرے جانور سے پورا کر لیا جاتا ہے تو ان دونوں کی جنس ایک ہے جیسے گائے اور بھینس اور اگر ایک جانور کا نصاب دوسرے جانور سے پورا نہیں کیا جاتا تو ان میں اختلاف جنس ہے جیسے بکری اور گائے۔
He said: And it is permissible to sell different meats, some for others, with disparity (tafāḍul), and he means camel meat, cow meat, and sheep/goat meat. As for cow and buffalo (jāmūs), they are one genus, and likewise goat (maʿz) with sheep (ḍā’n), and likewise ʿirāb (Arabian camel) with bukhtī (Bactrian camel). Translation: And selling different meats, some for others, with increase or decrease is permissible. His intent is camel meat, cow meat, and goat meat. And cow and buffalo are one genus, and similarly goat and sheep, and Arabian camel and Bactrian camel are one genus. Explanation: The case is that if the meats are of different genres, e.g., camel meat, cow meat, and goat meat, then selling one for another with increase or decrease is permissible, because the genre of cow, camel, and goat meat is separate. And when genres are different, selling with increase/decrease is permissible. But cow and buffalo are of one genus, so selling one for the other with increase/decrease is not permissible. Similarly, goat and sheep are of one genus, and Arabian and Bactrian camel are of one genus, so selling goat meat for sheep meat, and Arabian camel meat for Bactrian camel meat, with increase/decrease is not permissible. The author of ʿInāyah stated a principle regarding unity and difference of genus in animals: If the nisāb (minimum zakat threshold) of one animal can be completed by another animal, then they are of the same genus, like cow and buffalo; and if the nisāb of one cannot be completed by another, then there is difference in genus, like goat and cow.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hidāyah on exchanging different types of meat with disparity (tafāḍul). The general principle: if two items are of different genres, disparity is allowed; if they are of the same genus, equality is required. The text specifies that meats of different animal species (camel, cow, goat) are of different genres, so they can be exchanged in unequal quantities. However, within the same species (e.g., cow and buffalo, goat and sheep, Arabian and Bactrian camel), the meat is considered one genus, so inequality is not allowed due to ribā al-fadl. The passage then quotes ʿInāyah to provide a practical rule for determining genus in animals: if the nisāb of zakat can be completed by combining two animals, they are the same genus; otherwise, they are different. This rule is consistent with the Hanafi position on zakat and ribā. The student should note that the prohibition applies to ribā al-fadl in weighed/measured foods; meat is weighed, so same-genus meats must be equal when exchanged.

Key Terms
تفاضل (tafāḍul): Inequality in exchange; selling one item for more or less of the same genre or different genre.
جنس (jins): Genus; here determined by the rule of zakat nisāb completion from ʿInāyah.
نصاب (niṣāb): Minimum threshold for zakat liability; used here as a criterion for determining genus of animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, is it permissible to sell 1 kg of cow meat for 1.2 kg of buffalo meat?
A) Yes, because all meats are halal and can be exchanged freely.
B) No, because cow and buffalo meat are considered one genus, so equality is required.
C) Yes, because they are different animal species.
D) No, unless the meat is weighed and equal.

Key Takeaway
Disparity in meat exchange is allowed only when the meats are from different genera; genus is determined by whether the zakat nisāb of one animal can be completed by the other.

Sale of Fruits for Their Derivatives: Condition of Excess
Ruling on fruit-for-derivative exchange with condition of excess

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for the validity of selling a fruit for its processed derivative (oil, ghee, syrup) and identify when such a sale becomes impermissible due to riba al-fadl.

Core Idea
A fruit cannot be exchanged for its derivative unless the derivative is greater in quantity—otherwise it is riba.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a key ruling on barter transactions involving fruits and their derivatives. It clarifies that exchanging walnuts for walnut oil, milk for ghee, grapes for grape juice, or dates for date syrup is only permissible if the quantity of the derivative exceeds what can be extracted from the fruit. Otherwise, the sale is invalid due to riba al-fadl (usury of excess).

Guided Path
1
Identify the four examples
The text lists four specific transactions: walnut for oil, milk for ghee, grapes for grape juice, and dates for date syrup.
2
State the condition
For each transaction, the derivative (oil, ghee, juice, syrup) must be greater in quantity than the amount that can be extracted from the fruit being sold.
The condition is 'excess' (ziyadah) to avoid riba al-fadl.
3
Conclude the ruling
If the condition is met, the sale is permissible (ja'iz/sahih). If not, it is impermissible (haram/fasid) due to riba.
This ruling applies only to same-genus items; different genres have no such condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرماتے ہیں کہ اخروٹ کی بیع اس کے تیل کے عوض اور دودھ کی بیع اس کے گھی کے عوض اور انگور کی بیع شیرہ انگور کے عوض اور کھجور کی بیع اس کے شیرہ کے عوض اسی اعتبار پر جائز ہے یعنی اگر خالص تیل اس زائد ہو جو انگور میں ہے اور کھجور کا خالص شیرہ اس سے زائد ہو جو کھجور میں ہے تو یہ بیع جائز ہے ورنہ نہیں۔
He says: The sale of walnuts for its oil, and milk for its ghee, and grapes for grape juice, and dates for its syrup is permissible on this basis: if the pure oil exceeds what is in the grapes, and the pure syrup of dates exceeds what is in the dates, then this sale is permissible; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This is a ruling regarding the exchange of a fruit or its product for its processed derivative (like oil, ghee, syrup). The condition is that the derivative must be more in quantity than what can be extracted from the original item, otherwise it would be considered riba because of same genus. The text mentions specific examples: walnuts for oil, milk for ghee, grapes for grape syrup, dates for date syrup. The ruling is that such a sale is only valid if the pure derivative (e.g., oil) is in a quantity greater than what is contained in the fruit being sold. If the derivative is equal or less, the sale is invalid due to riba al-fadl in same genus items. This is a typical Hanafi rule in the chapter of sales concerning riba.

Key Terms
جائز (ja'iz): Permissible in the sense of legally allowed, but conditional.
زائد (za'id): Excess quantity that ensures no riba al-fadl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, when is the sale of grapes for grape juice permissible?
A) Always permissible regardless of quantity
B) Permissible only if the juice quantity exceeds the amount extractable from the grapes
C) Permissible only if the juice is equal in weight
D) Never permissible

Key Takeaway
The sale of a fruit for its processed derivative is valid only when the derivative is in greater quantity than what can be extracted from the fruit; otherwise, it is forbidden due to riba al-fadl.

Cotton-for-Thread Sale: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Genus and Riba
Presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on cotton-thread exchange.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on selling cotton for its thread by weight, identify the proofs for each position, and apply the analogy to other processed goods.

Core Idea
Cotton and thread share genus but processing changes weight — two Hanafi positions exist.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, a disagreement among Hanafi scholars is presented regarding the permissibility of exchanging raw cotton for its spun thread in equal weight. The core question is whether the transformation from cotton to thread alters the genus (jins) for purposes of riba al-fadl. Some scholars permit it based on unity of origin and measure, while others prohibit it by analogy to wheat and flour, where processing reduces weight. This lesson unpacks both arguments and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Issue
Identify the specific transaction under dispute: selling raw cotton for its own spun thread, both by equal weight.
The sale involves two items from the same raw material, both weighable.
2
Identify the Two Positions
Note that some mashayikh permit the sale, others prohibit it. Both are recognized positions within the Hanafi school.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab one.
3
Analyze the Proof for Permissibility
The permissibility argument relies on unity of genus (ittihad al-jins) – both stem from cotton – and unity of measure (both weighable).
These two factors normally make an exchange subject to riba rules, but equal weight avoids riba.
4
Analyze the Proof for Prohibition
The prohibition argument uses qiyas to wheat and flour: spinning reduces weight, so equal weight exchange is inequitable, similar to wheat flour weighing less than the original wheat.
The analogy highlights that processing changes the substance and weight.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference is whether a person can directly exchange raw cotton for thread of equal weight without falling into riba.
If permitted, the exchange is valid; if prohibited, it must be avoided or done with inequality known to be fair.

صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ روئی کو اس کے سوت کے عوض وزن کے اعتبار سے برابر کر کے بیچنے میں اختلاف ہے چنانچہ بعض مشائخ جواز کے قائل ہیں اور جواز کی دلیل یہ بیان کرتے ہیں کہ روئی اور سوت دونوں کی اصل ایک ہے یعنی روئی کی اصل بھی کپاس ہے اور سوت کی اصل بھی کپاس ہے اور دونوں موزونی ہیں ۔ پس اتحاد جنس اور اتحاد قد ر دونوں پائے گئے ۔ اسلئے روئی کوسوت کے عوض برابر ، برابر بیچنے میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے۔ اور بعض مشائخ کا قول ہے کہ روئی کوسوت کے عوض برابر کر کے بیچنا بھی جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ روئی کو کات کر سوت بنانے سے روئی کم ہو جاتی ہے۔ مثلاً ایک کلو روئی کو کات کر اگر سوت بنایا گیا تو یہ ایک کلو نہ ہوگا بلکہ ایک کلو سے کم ہو جائے گا۔ جیسے گندم کو آٹے کے عوض برابر ، برابر بیچنا جائز نہیں ہے اور سوتی کپڑے کو روئی کے عوض بیچنا ہر طرح جائز ہے خواہ کپڑے کی روئی سے یہ روئی زیادہ ہو۔ خواہ برابر ہو خواہ کم ہو۔ اور اس پر اجماع ہے کیونکہ سوتی کپڑے اور سوئی کی جنس مختلف ہیں اس لئے کمی ، زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنے میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے۔
The author of al-Hidayah says: There is disagreement regarding the sale of cotton for its thread by equal weight. Some mashayikh (scholars) deem it permissible, and their proof is that cotton and thread have the same origin—both come from cotton—and both are weighable. Therefore, unity of genus and unity of measure exist, so there is no harm in selling cotton for thread equally. Other scholars say it is not permissible because when cotton is spun into thread, it loses weight. For example, if one kilo of cotton is spun into thread, it will not be one kilo but less. Similarly, selling wheat for flour equally is not permissible. However, selling cotton cloth for cotton is permissible in all cases, whether the cloth's cotton is more, equal, or less, and there is consensus on this because cloth and raw cotton are of different genera, so disparity in quantity is allowed.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding the sale of cotton for its thread by equal weight. The issue is whether they are considered the same genus (jins) and thus subject to riba al-fadl rules. Some scholars argue that since both originate from the same raw material (cotton) and are both measured by weight, they are the same genus; therefore, selling them in equal quantity is permissible. Others disagree, pointing out that spinning reduces the weight of cotton, so equal-weight exchange would mean the cotton is effectively sold for less than its own weight after processing, analogous to wheat and flour. The passage also notes that sale of cotton cloth (a manufactured product) for raw cotton is unanimously permissible because they differ in genus (due to manufacturing change). This shows a nuanced application of the concept of jins in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
اتحاد جنس (ittihad al-jins): Unity of genus; items belonging to same category for riba purposes.
موزونی (mawzuni): Weighable items subject to riba rules when exchanged with same genus.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to extend a ruling from an established case to a new case due to shared effective cause (illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the reason given by those who prohibit selling cotton for its thread by equal weight?
A) Cotton and thread are different genera
B) Spinning reduces weight, making equal exchange unfair
C) Thread is not a weighable item
D) There is consensus on prohibition

Key Takeaway
Within the Hanafi school, there is disagreement on whether processing changes the genus for riba purposes, reflecting a nuanced application of the concept of jins.

Selling Meats with Disparity: Genus Rule in Hanafi Fiqh
Rules on exchange of different types of meat with inequality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on riba al-fadl in meat exchange and apply the zakat nisab principle to determine whether two meats are of the same or different genus.

Core Idea
Meats of different genera can be exchanged with disparity; same genus requires equality to avoid riba.

When exchanging meats, the Hanafi school distinguishes genera by animal species. Meats from different species (camel, cow, goat) may be sold with disparity, but meats within the same species or its subtypes (cow/buffalo, goat/sheep, Arabian/Bactrian camel) must be exchanged weight for weight to prevent riba al-fadl. This ruling is grounded in a principle derived from zakat: if the nisab of one animal can be completed by another in zakat payment, they are one genus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Scope
The text states that selling different meats with disparity is permissible (jāʾiz). Your task is to recognise that this permissibility is conditional on the meats being of different genera.
The ruling applies to exchanges of meat for meat, not to barter of live animals.
2
Distinguish Same vs. Different Genera
The author lists examples: camel, cow, and goat are different genera; cow and buffalo are same genus; goat and sheep are same genus; Arabian and Bactrian camels are same genus. Understand that subtypes within a species are considered one genus.
The criterion is not just external similarity but a principle from zakat (see next step).
3
Apply the Zakat Nisab Principle
The text cites al-Inayah: if the nisab (minimum threshold) of one animal can be completed by another in zakat, they are same genus. For example, cows and buffaloes share zakat rules, so they are one genus; goats and camels do not, so they differ.
This principle extends to meat, milk, and other products of these animals.
4
Conclude the Legal Consequence
If meats are different genera, disparity (fadl) is allowed; if same genus, inequivalent exchange is prohibited due to riba. This ensures the rule is applied consistently.
Remember: the prohibition is on unequal exchange within the same genus, not on the act of selling itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مختلف گوشتوں کی آپس میں کمی زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچ کا حکم
قال ويجوز بيع اللحمان المختلفة بعضها ببعض متفاضلا ومراده لحم الإبل والبقر والغنم فأما البقر والجواميس جنس واحد وكذا المعز مع الضان وكذا العراب مع البخاتي
ترجمہ اور مختلف گوشتوں میں سے بعض کو بعض کے عوض کمی ، زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنا جائز ہے اور اس کی مراد اونٹ کا گوشت ، گائے کا گوشت اور بکری کا گوشت ہے۔ اور گائے اور بھینس کا گوشت ایک ہی جنس ہے۔ اور اسی طرح بکری اور بھیڑ اور عربی اور بختی ایک ہی جنس ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر گوشت مختلف الا جناس ہوں مثلاً اونٹ کا گوشت ہے، گائے کا گوشت ہے اور بکری کا گوشت ہے۔ ان میں سے اگر ایک کو دوسرے کے عوض کمی ، زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچا گیا تو جائز ہے کیونکہ گائے ، اونٹ اور بکری کے گوشت کی جنس الگ الگ ہے۔ اور اختلاف جنس کی صورت میں چونکہ کمی ، زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنا جائز ہو گا۔ اوگائے اور بھینس دونوں کی ایک جنس ہے لہذا ان میں سے ایک کے گوشت کو دوسرے کے عوض کمی ، زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنا جائز نہیں ہے اور اسی طرح بکری اور بھیٹر کی ایک جنس ہے اور عربی اونٹ اور بختی اونٹ کی ایک جنس ہے تو بکری کے گوشت کو بھیٹر کے گوشت کے عوض اور عربی اونٹ کے گوشت کو بختی اونٹ کے گوشت کے عوض کمی ، زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ صاحب عنایہ نے حیوانات میں اتحاد جنس اور اختلاف جنس کے سلسلہ میں ایک ضابطہ بیان فرمایا ہے وہ یہ کہ زکوۃ میں اگر ایک جانور کا نصاب دوسرے جانور سے پورا کر لیا جاتا ہے تو ان دونوں کی جنس ایک ہے جیسے گائے اور بھینس اور اگر ایک جانور کا نصاب دوسرے جانور سے پورا نہیں کیا جاتا تو ان میں اختلاف جنس ہے جیسے بکری اور گائے ۔
Ruling on selling different meats with disparity. He said: It is permissible to sell different meats one for another with disparity, and he means camel meat, cow meat, and goat meat. As for cow and buffalo, they are one genus; similarly, goat and sheep, and Arabian camels and Bactrian camels are one genus. Translation: And selling some of the different meats for others with disparity is permissible, and his intention is camel meat, cow meat, and goat meat. And cow and buffalo meat are one genus. Similarly, goat and sheep, and Arabian and Bactrian are one genus. Explanation: The issue is that if meats are of different genera, e.g., camel, cow, and goat meat, then selling one for another with disparity is permissible because their genera are separate. When genera differ, disparity is allowed. Cow and buffalo are one genus, so selling one for the other with disparity is not permissible. Similarly, goat and sheep are one genus, and Arabian and Bactrian camels are one genus, so selling goat meat for sheep meat or Arabian camel meat for Bactrian camel meat with disparity is not permissible. The author of al-Inayah stated a principle regarding unity and difference of genus in animals: if the nisab of one animal can be completed by the other in zakat, then they are one genus, like cow and buffalo; if not, they differ, like goat and cow.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on exchanging different types of meat with disparity (fadl). The key principle is that meats of distinct animal species (camel, cow, goat) are considered different genera, so exchanging them with disparity is permissible. However, subtypes within a species (cow vs buffalo, goat vs sheep, Arabian vs Bactrian camel) are considered one genus, so selling with disparity is not permitted due to riba al-fadl. The text also cites a principle from al-Inayah: in zakat, if the nisab of one animal can be completed by another, they are same genus; otherwise, they differ. This principle helps determine genus for meat and milk rulings. The ruling is consistent with the Hanafi position on riba in food items.

Key Terms
لحم (lahm): Meat; considered as a food item subject to riba rules.
نصاب (nisab): Minimum threshold for zakat obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following pairs of meats are considered the same genus?
A) Camel and cow meat
B) Cow and buffalo meat
C) Goat and camel meat
D) Goat and cow meat

Key Takeaway
Meats of different animal species (determined by zakat nisab criteria) can be exchanged with disparity; same-genus meats require equality to avoid riba.

Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Selling Different Milks with Disparity
Inter-madhhab dispute on milk genus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on selling milk of different animals with disparity and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the principle of usul and furu'.

Core Idea
Different origins (cow, goat) make milk different genera; disparity is allowed unless processed.

This lesson delves into a classic inter-madhhab dispute about the sale of milk from different animals. The Hanafi school, relying on the principle that parts (furu') follow their origins (usul), permits selling cow's milk and goat's milk with disparity. However, Imam al-Shafi'i holds that all milk is one genus because it serves a common purpose—nourishment. The passage from al-Hidayah clarifies that the genus difference holds only when the milk is unprocessed. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify how different usul al-fiqh axioms lead to divergent rulings in mu'amalat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position and its core principle
State that Hanafi law permits selling milk of different animals with disparity. Note that the key principle is that furu' (milk) follow the usul (animals) in determining genus.
The principle is derived from zakat: the nisab of one animal cannot be completed by another of a different species.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and its core principle
State that Imam al-Shafi'i prohibits disparity because he considers all milk one genus based on shared purpose (ta'widh al-magsud).
This is a difference in how to define genus: origin vs. purpose.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is that in zakat, the nisab of cows is not completed by goats, proving they are different genera. This difference in origin necessitates difference in their parts (milk).
The proof is based on a legal analogy (qiyas) from zakat law to sales.
4
Understand the exception for processed milk
Note that the author of al-Hidayah adds that if milk is processed (e.g., made into cheese), it becomes one genus, and disparity is not allowed.
Processing changes the inherent nature, overriding the origin-based distinction.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, one can exchange 1 liter cow milk for 2 liters goat milk; under Shafi'i, only equal exchange is valid.
This affects everyday transactions among Muslims of different madhahib.

Source Text

بکری اور گائے کے دودھ کی آپس میں کمی زیادتی کے ساتھ بیع کا حکم
قال كذالك ألبان البقر والغنم وعن الشافعى لايجوز؛ لأنها جنس واحد لاتحاد المقصود ولنا أن الأصول مختلفة حتى لايكمل نصاب أحدهما بالآخر فى الزكوة فكذا أجزائها إذا لم تتبدل الصنعة
ترجمہ اور اسی طرح گائے اور بکری کا دودھ ہے اور امام شافعی سے مروی ہے کہ جائز نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ سب دودہ مقصود کے متحد ہونے کی وجہ سے ایک ہی جنس ہیں۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ ان جانورں کی اصل مختلف ہیں حتی کہ زکوۃ میں ان میں سے ایک کا نصاب دوسرے سے پورا نہیں کیا جاتا ہے۔ پس اسی طرح ان کے جزاء ہیں جبکہ کسی کا ریگری کے ذریعہ ان میں تبدل نہ ہوا ہو۔ تشریح شیخ ابوالحسن قدوری فرماتے ہیں کہ اونٹنی ، گائے اور بکری کے دودھ میں بھی اختلاف جنس ہے لہذا ایک کا دودھ دوسرے کے دودھ کے عوض کمی ، زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنا جائز ہے۔ لیکن حضرت امام شافعی سے ایک روایت یہ ہے کہ ان میں سے ایک کے گوشت کو دوسرے کے عوض اور ایک کے دودھ کو دوسرے کے دودھ کے عوض کمی ، زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنا جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ تمام کے گوشت کی ایک جنس ہے اور تمام کے دودھ کی ایک جنس ہے اور ایک جنس اسلئے ہے کہ سب کا مقصد ایک ہے یعنی غذا حاصل کرنا یا تقویت حاصل کرنا پس جب سب کی جنس ایک ہے تو اونٹ، گائے ، بکری میں سے ایک کے گوشت کو دوسرے کے گوشت کے عوض اور ایک کے دودھ کو دوسرے کے دودھ کے عوض برابر، برابر بیچنا تو جائز ہے لیکن کمی ، زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنا جائز نہیں ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ گوشت اور دودھ فروع اور اجزاء ہیں اور ان کے اصول یعنی اونٹ، گائے اور بکری مختلفہ الا جناس ہیں اور اصول کا اختلاف فروع کے اختلاف کو واجب کرتا ہے۔ پس جب یہ اصول یعنی اونٹ، گائے اور بکری مختلفۃ الاجناس ہیں حتی کہ ان میں سے ایک کا نصاب زکوۃ دوسرے کے ذریعہ پورا نہیں کیا جاتا تو ان کے فروع اور اجزاء یعنی دودھ اور گوشت بھی مختلفۃ الاجناس ہوں گے اور جب یہ مختلفته الاجناس ہیں تو ایک کا گوشت دوسرے کے گوشت کے عوض اور ایک کا دودھ دوسرے کے دودھ کے عوض کمی ، زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنا جائز ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اونٹنی ، گائے اور بکری کا دودھ اس وقت تک مختلفۃ الاجناس میں جب تک کہ کاریگری کے ذریعہ ان میں تبدیلی نہ کی گئی ہو یعنی دودھ ہوں اور اگر تبدیلی کر دی گئی مثلاً مینوں کے دودھ کا پنیر بنالیا تو اب یہ ایک ہی جنس شمار ہوگا اور کی زیادتی کے ساتھ بیچنا جائز نہ ہوگا اگر چہ اصل یعنی دودھ مختلفۃ الاجناس ہیں۔
Ruling on selling milk of goat and cow with disparity. He said: Similarly for the milk of cows and goats (the ruling applies). And from al-Shafi'i it is narrated that it is not permissible because they are one genus due to unity of purpose. Our proof: the origins (animals) are different, so much so that the nisab of one is not completed by the other in zakat; thus their parts (milk, meat) are likewise different unless processing changes them. Explanation: Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Quduri says that the milk of camel, cow, and goat differ in genus, so selling one for another with disparity is permissible. However, Imam al-Shafi'i has a narration that it is not permissible because all meat is one genus and all milk is one genus due to common purpose (nourishment). Thus, if all are same genus, selling equal for equal is permissible but not with disparity. Our proof: meat and milk are parts (furu') and their origins (animals) are different in genus; difference in origin necessitates difference in parts. Since the origins (camel, cow, goat) are different (their nisabs are not completed by each other), their parts (milk, meat) are also different genera, so selling with disparity is permissible. The author of al-Hidayah says: the milk of camel, cow, and goat are different genera as long as they are not processed; if processed (e.g., made into cheese), they become one genus and selling with disparity is not permissible.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents the inter-madhhab dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i regarding the sale of milk from different animals (cow, goat, camel) with disparity. The Hanafi position, based on Imam Abu Hanifa and supported by al-Quduri, is that these milks are of different genera because their sources (animals) are distinct species, as evidenced by zakat rules (nisab of one does not complete the other). Therefore, disparity is allowed. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that milk has a common purpose (nourishment), making it one genus, so disparity is not allowed. The Hanafi response emphasizes the principle that the difference in origins (usul) mandates difference in parts (furu'). Additionally, a key exception is noted: if milk is processed (e.g., into cheese), the genus changes, and they become one genus regardless of origin. This shows the Hanafi reliance on the principle of usul and furu in determining genus for riba purposes. The passage also includes a comment from al-Hidayah emphasizing the role of manufacturing change.

Key Terms
أصول (usul): Origins; the base animals from which milk/meat are derived.
فروع (furu'): Parts or products derived from the origin (e.g., milk, meat).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what is the primary reason that milk of cow and goat are different genera?
A) They have different tastes
B) Their origins (animals) are different, as shown in zakat rules
C) They are processed differently
D) The Prophet (peace be upon him) said so

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school differentiates milk genera by animal origin; milk from different species is considered different genera unless processed, allowing disparity in exchange.
